published by Agri-Pulse, the Washington, D.C.-based ag news service, the top bosses at ag-centered commodity groups and federally-chartered checkoff agencies had far better recent years than the farmers and ranchers they claim to serve. For example, Agri-Pulse&rsquo;s annual compensation report published last September noted that Steve Censky, chief executive officer of the American Soybean Association, received $341,663 in total compensation in 2015. If that sounds like a lot of dough for a commodity organization to shell out for a hired hand, it&rsquo;s peanuts compared to other soybean group CEOs. Jim Sutter, the chief of the U.S. Soybean Export Council, took home $435,944 in total compensation in 2014, the latest available numbers. John Becherer, the CEO of the United Soybean Board, the operational arm of the mandatory, non-refundable soybean checkoff, did even better; he earned $464,208 in 2015. The bean bosses, however, were pikers compared to the livestock boys. For instance, in 2014, Tom Gallagher, CEO of Dairy Management Inc., the operating structure over most of the dairy checkoff, was paid $618,910 in salary alone. By contrast, Polly Ruhland, boss at the beef checkoff&rsquo;s Cattlemen&rsquo;s Beef Promotion & Research Board, earned a skinny $287,500 in 2015. Meanwhile, the former boss of the almost totally checkoff-dependent National Cattlemen&rsquo;s Beef Association (NCBA), Forrest Roberts, took home nearly twice as much as Ruhland, $548,993, in total compensation in 2014, the year before he rode off into a comfortable sunset. Also, Phil Seng, the longtime president and CEO of the U.S. Meat Export Federation, which receives hefty funding from several livestock checkoffs, earned $461,630 in total pay that same year. By contrast, Wayne Pacelle, the top dog at Humane Society of the U.S. that most livestock groups love to kick, earned but $425,283 in 2015. The pork checkoff&rsquo;s National Pork Board paid Bill Even, its new CEO, $400,000 in total 2016 compensation last year even though, as Agri-Pulse reported, he worked but a &ldquo;partial year.&rdquo; And, two years ago &mdash; again, the latest data available &mdash; Neil Dierks, CEO of the National Pork Producers Council (NPPC), received $304,384 in total compensation. If all these commodity and checkoff organizations, titles, and salaries appear the same, it&rsquo;s only because these groups&rsquo; structure, pay, and locations have grown to be oddly similar. For example, it could be just an interesting coincidence that the National Pork Board (the checkoff) and pork&rsquo;s most powerful lobbying group, the NPPC, are located just two miles &mdash; or six minutes by car &mdash; apart on the western edge of Des Moines. It could be coincidence, right? Then again, when you Google &ldquo;NPPC,&rdquo; you discover that the group is &ldquo;Based in Washington, D.C.,&rdquo; not Iowa. Reinforcing that confusion, NPPC&rsquo;s Facebook and Twitter profiles list Washington, D.C. as its headquarters, not metro Des Moines. The pork boys aren&rsquo;t the only neighborly ag groups in America. The United Soybean Board (the checkoff) is located just one floor from one of its key contractors, the U.S. Soybean Export Council, in the same suburban St. Louis, MO office building. The cowboys are only slightly different. The beef checkoff&rsquo;s office in Centennial, CO is separated from its largest contractor, the NCBA, by one street. Google maps estimates the walk between the two groups&rsquo; offices at a barnyard-ambling three minutes, or 175 yards &mdash; and that&rsquo;s if you take the long way, the sidewalks, and not the backyard-to-backyard lawn. At one time, coincidences like these mattered. Now, however, today&rsquo;s cross-pollination of mandatory commodity checkoffs with voluntary commodity groups often works more to promote each others&rsquo; livelihood and six-figure staff salaries than your livelihood and declining salaries. The proof is in the numbers. As &ldquo;CEO compensation continues to climb upward,&rdquo; noted the headline on the Agri-Pulse compensation report, U.S. net farm income continued to tumble &mdash; from $124 billion in 2013 to a forecasted $62 billion in 2017. That&rsquo;s a 50 percent crash; just to put a number on it. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/local/commentary-numbers-aren-adding-farm-income/iqYD3uZEg4Yq9aplbXgNWJ/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/SpringfieldNewsSun/2017/04/22/Images/newsEngin.18349425_guebertcrop.jpg.jpg\" title=\"Commentary: Numbers aren’t adding up on farm income \" alt=\"Commentary: Numbers aren’t adding up on farm income \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/local/commentary-numbers-aren-adding-farm-income/iqYD3uZEg4Yq9aplbXgNWJ/\" target=\"\">Commentary: Numbers aren’t adding up on farm income </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nAlan Guebert</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate is-truncated\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>According to numbers compiled and published by Agri-Pulse, the Washington, D.C.-based ag news serve.</div></div>\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t</article>\n\n\t\t            \n\t\t        \n            \n        \n    \n    \n    \n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n \n\n<!-- Rendering News of the killings a year ago today caught the eye of the nation for a moment. But as the cameras, satellite trucks and reporters left, what remained was a family in pain and a $10,000 reward for the tip to end their nightmare. The video &mdash; announced moments before its release by Attorney General Mike DeWine on WHIO Radio&rsquo;s Miami Valley Morning News &mdash; is a plea from the family for answers and more donations to the Crime Stoppers reward. For nearly 30 minutes, Rhoden and Grebing describe their loss in what DeWine called &ldquo;gut-wrenching&rdquo; detail. &ldquo;Please, please, please come forward,&rdquo; Grebing says with increasing emotion, crackling the audio. Between sobs, she expresses comfort in knowing her lost family members would make the same plea for them had fate cast a different lot. She professes hope in knowing some day &ldquo;the case will be cracked and we will have answers.&rdquo; &ldquo;Please end this nightmare we&rsquo;ve been living,&rdquo; she says. The Rhodens &mdash; Christopher Sr., 40, Christopher Jr., 16, Dana, 37, Gary, 38, Hanna, 19, Kenneth, 44, and 20-year-old Frankie &mdash; died April 22, 2016, with Frankie&rsquo;s fiancee, Hannah Gilley, 20, in four homes across Pike County. DeWine told WHIO&rsquo;s Larry Hansgen it is &ldquo;hard to tell&rdquo; if would-be tipsters are withholding information more due to fear of prosecution or retaliation for speaking out. But he doesn&rsquo;t &ldquo;think there&rsquo;s any doubt &hellip; there are people who know things they are simply not telling us.&rdquo; &ldquo;Anyone who has information and is afraid to give it to us because it might implicate them in some growing of marijuana or some sort of involvement in drugs, we want to assure them our focus is not on that,&rdquo; DeWine said. &ldquo;Our focus is on eight human beings who have been killed.&rdquo; But still, Pike County Sheriff Charles Reader said the fear of becoming another victim exists. &ldquo;The fear of retaliation from whoever these killers are, that&rsquo;s real,&rdquo; he said. Anyone with information on the murders is asked to call the Ohio Bureau of Criminal Investigation, 1-855-BCI-OHIO (224-6446) or the Pike County Sheriff&rsquo;s Office at 740-947-2111.     \n            \n            \n            \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  SPRINGFIELD &ndash; About two decades ago, at the age of 49, Springfield native Joan Balium was at a crossroads in her life. With three children and a special needs grandson to finish raising, and suddenly no house to raise them in, Balium was searching for answers. She found them at the local Habitat For Humanity chapter, which two years ago became the Clark County Fuller Center for Housing. RELATED: Clark County housing organization parts ways with national group The result of that meeting was Balium becoming a first-time home owner in 1997 despite her low income, a home she just paid off in February. She is just one of many success stories authored by the Fuller Center&rsquo;s non-profit Christian ministry, stories which will be celebrated at its annual Spring Celebration dinner from 5 to 9 p.m. April 29 at the Clark County Fairgrounds. The Fuller Center&rsquo;s mission is to eliminate substandard housing and poverty with the building or remodeling of homes in low-income neighborhoods, using mostly volunteer labor and donated or discounted materials, then qualifying low-income families for zero-interest loans on no-profit houses so that they can realize their dream of home ownership. &ldquo;I never gave up. I knew I would own a home somehow,&rdquo; said Balium, now 68 years old. &ldquo;I also believed in the Lord. He kind of gave me a head&rsquo;s up that I would get a home one day. I just stuck with it and waited on His timing. He delivered, and He is still watching over me.&rdquo; It may not have seemed that way back then. But then again, she and her family were not injured when the house she was renting on Miller Street burned to the ground. Homeless and living with her daughter, the answer just popped up right in front of her eyes one day. &ldquo;I saw a billboard about Habitat, so I looked into what it would take to get a home,&rdquo; Balium said. RELATED: Clark County housing center gets new leader, looks to retool in 2017 The next thing she knew, she was helping to build her own home and others as part of the &ldquo;sweat equity&rdquo; all families do before moving into their home. &ldquo;I was mechanically inclined, more than most ladies, and that helped,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;I helped do two homes on Light Street, a home on Fair Street, and another couple of homes on the north side. I helped build other homes, then helped build mine.&rdquo; She enjoyed working other people, so much so that she continued to volunteer long after her sweat equity hours were finished. Learning the ins and outs of home repairs has helped her as a home owner, she said. &ldquo;I always believed there is value in a home, and in owning your own place,&rdquo; said Balium. She also became an example for others to follow. &ldquo;If they know you did it, someone else might try,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;They don&rsquo;t see themselves as builders. But you don&rsquo;t necessarily have to swing a hammer to do your sweat equity.&rdquo; It also allowed Balium to build her nest egg with the fundamental building block &mdash; real estate that only appreciates in value. &ldquo;I never really wanted that much,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;I loved the (low) mortgage payments. For me, it made it easier to pay for it. Some of the rents are outrageous. And then you have to pay utilities, trash. I always felt that whatever you could do would help you in the long run.&rdquo; And it has. Originally, her zero-interest loan was for 30 years, but she paid it off in 20. And she did it while being on a fixed income all the way through. Now Balium has something to say to those who have given up on the idea of being a home owner. &ldquo;I would tell them to check it out; it may just be what they are looking for,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;For me, payments were easier. They work with you to help you to qualify. The people who built it were serious about, they didn&rsquo;t do any half-jobs. They built good homes.&rdquo; And she is now the proud outright owner of one of those homes. &ldquo;It feels real good,&rdquo; said Balium. &ldquo;Now I&rsquo;m looking for the next adventure, which might be a garage.&rdquo; If you or someone you know is interested in becoming a Fuller Center for Housing homeowner or supporting the non-profit ministry, call (937) 325-2514. Kermit Rowe is the executive director of the Clark County Fuller Center for Housing. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/local/clark-county-fuller-center-celebrate-success-stories/FPkELi9SEETFt7ChVdnjNP/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/SpringfieldNewsSun/2017/04/21/Images/newsEngin.18468071_011217-fuller-center-1.jpg\" title=\"Clark County Fuller Center to celebrate success stories \" alt=\"Clark County Fuller Center to celebrate success stories \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/local/clark-county-fuller-center-celebrate-success-stories/FPkELi9SEETFt7ChVdnjNP/\" target=\"\">Clark County Fuller Center to celebrate success stories </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nKermit Rowe, <span class=\"story-source\">Guest columnist</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate is-truncated\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>SPRINGFIELD – About two decades ago, at the age of 49, Springfield native Joan Balium was at a crossroads in her life.        \n    \n<!-- map content:  What interests the children in your life? Perhaps science and technology fascinate your nephew; your granddaughter enjoys playing team sports; your neighbor&rsquo;s son wants to be a drummer, and you know all too well from hearing him practice next door! All children have interests; however, not all children have an equal opportunity to pursue them. It is a heartbreaking fact that not all families can afford to pay participation fees or cover the costs of equipment or gear for clubs, sports or classes. RELATED: Springfield applies for federal Promise Zone status Does this mean the children of lower-income or single parent families are less deserving of opportunities to explore the world, to follow their dreams or to discover their passions? Absolutely not. As a result, Springfield Promise Neighborhood, a local nonprofit dedicated to the success of all children, has begun a Promise Zone Community Chest. Announced April 6 during the organization&rsquo;s annual Visioning Dinner event, the Community Chest will assist Springfield Promise Zone families by granting scholarships for costs and fees related to extended-learning activities for youth. The Promise Zone is a geographical area made up of four of the lowest income neighborhoods in Springfield: those surrounding Kenwood, Lincoln, Perrin Woods and Fulton Elementary schools. RELATED: Group performs, inspires Springfield youth to go to college The United States Department of Agriculture defines Promise Zones as communities high in poverty that have partnered with community leaders and the federal government to improve education and economic activity, while reducing violent crime and addressing other community priorities. The funds collected through the Promise Zone Community Chest will be held by Springfield Promise Neighborhood and managed by community residents. But without the support of like-minded individuals who believe in caring for all children, it will not flourish. Will you contribute a small amount, $5 or $10, to our cause? Just as importantly, we also ask that you spread the word about the Community Chest by telling five friends, family members or colleagues about this opportunity to give. Ask that they donate a small sum and continue to spread the word. Together, we will form a web of hope and promise that will benefit our biggest asset, our future, our children. Tracy Borders handles communication for Springfield Promise Neighborhood, and is with AmeriCorps VISTA. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/local/promise-zone-community-chest-help-cover-participation-costs/zjiKGIYY1oH7VGhYTUTdvI/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/SpringfieldNewsSun/2017/04/21/Images/newsEngin.18468205_032617-homicides-5.jpg\" title=\"Promise Zone Community Chest to help cover participation costs \" alt=\"Promise Zone Community Chest to help cover participation costs \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/local/promise-zone-community-chest-help-cover-participation-costs/zjiKGIYY1oH7VGhYTUTdvI/\" target=\"\">Promise Zone Community Chest to help cover participation costs </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nTracy Borders, <span class=\"story-source\">Guest columnist</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate is-truncated\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>What interests the children in your life? LEADS does not include data and files separately collected and maintained by intrastate regional systems or other individual user systems.&nbsp; LEADS utilizes out-of-state inquiries about persons, vehicles or licenses. Husted for Ohio campaign. Josh Eck said his last day in the office was today and he will become a spokesman for the campaign. &ldquo;I have been a fan of Jon Husted&rsquo;s since before I worked for him. He&rsquo;s been pretty clear that he&rsquo;s looking at what the future holds for him and I want to be supportive of that future however I can,&rdquo; Eck said. &ldquo;I think very highly of him and I think there are a lot of people in Ohio that think very highly of him.&rdquo; Husted formerly represented the Kettering area in the Ohio House of Representatives and Senate and also served as House Speaker. He is one of several Republicans and Democrats who are expected to run in 2018. &ldquo;I think he will be making a decision here very shortly,&rdquo; Eck said. This week political analyst Larry Sabato&rsquo;s Crystal Ball publication said Ohio leans Republican for 2018 and that the GOP has a &ldquo;strong bench&rdquo; with Husted, Ohio Attorney General Mike DeWine, Lt. Gov. Mary Taylor and U.S. Rep. Jim Renacci, R-Wadsworth, all possible contenders. Democrats who have said they are running or are interested include Dayton Mayor Nan Whaley, former state representative Connie Pillich of Montgomery, state Sen. Joe Schiavoni, D-Boardman and former U.S. Rep. Betty Sutton of Copely. &ldquo;Sutton and Whaley are probably the most credible candidates in that group,&rdquo; according to the Sabato report, published by the University of Virginia Center for Politics. There is speculation that Dennis Kucinich, a former congressman and ex-Cleveland mayor, would throw his hat into the Democratic primary as well. He is appearing in Columbus and in Washington Twp. on Monday. He will talk about the privatization of public education and is not expected to announce a bid for governor at that time, said Andrew Wilson, a spokesman for Public Education Partners, which is organizing the local visit.   \n<!-- map content:  The Clark-Shawnee Local School District has completed its internal investigation into the school&rsquo;s baseball team and said students will face consequences. The district has been investigating the Shawnee High School baseball program for about three weeks after allegations were made involving student athletes during a spring break baseball trip to Tennessee. Superintendent Gregg Morris said Friday he couldn&rsquo;t say what happened during the trip or what actions the district has taken due to federal privacy laws. &ldquo;At this time we have issued athletic and academic consequences,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Privacy laws do limit what we&rsquo;re able to comment about around the investigation but we do appreciate the families and students for all their help throughout this process. Right now we&rsquo;re focused on providing support to our students.&rdquo; A volunteer coach and three baseball players had been suspended earlier following the investigation. The coach now has been dismissed, Morris said, but he wouldn&rsquo;t say whether the three students were removed from the team. Morris declined Friday to identify the players or the coach. &ldquo;It was a supervision issue,&rdquo; Morris said of the volunteer coach&rsquo;s dismissal. &ldquo;We have high standards for students and we have higher standards for those who are supervising our students and that was the issue we are addressing.&rdquo; The team was supervised by both coaches and parent chaperones on the trip. When asked if he was satisfied with the parental supervision, Morris said, &ldquo;We addressed every issue that we saw that needed to be addressed.&rdquo; The district will take a deeper look into its policies regarding school trips, Morris said. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re evaluating the whole process for trips and I&rsquo;m not implying in any way that there will be no more trips,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re going to review our procedures for trips and we&rsquo;re probably going to tighten some of those. As parents and students come back for the next school year and we have our initial meetings with our students and their parents, that will be an emphasis during those discussions.&rdquo; The Shawnee varsity and junior varsity teams missed a combined 10 games while the school was investigating the allegations. He said he made the decision to cancel the games for student safety. &ldquo;The reason they weren&rsquo;t playing was I wanted to be assured and understand the situation well enough to assure safety &hellip; once I was assured of that, that&rsquo;s when we continued the season,&rdquo; Morris said. An administrator will be present at all remaining games in the season, he said. The Clark County Sheriff&rsquo;s Office is also conducting an investigation into the alleged incidents. Chief Deputy Travis Russell said Friday that the office continues to investigate and declined to give more information. A sheriff&rsquo;s report states that the office is investigating harassment claims. A parent who asked not to be identified has told the Springfield News-Sun that her son was on the baseball trip and described the allegations as an assault. Her son is one of the victims, she said. The mother said she believes the alleged assaults were a type of initiation and her son and other students weren&rsquo;t given a choice. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/local-education/shawnee-wraps-baseball-investigation-students-face-consequences/k9FLE0M4thJbl373Zy76vM/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image/Pub/p8/WHIO/2017/04/21/Videos/4042658.vpx\" title=\"Shawnee wraps up baseball investigation, students face ‘consequences’ \" alt=\"Shawnee wraps up baseball investigation, students face ‘consequences’ \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/local-education/shawnee-wraps-baseball-investigation-students-face-consequences/k9FLE0M4thJbl373Zy76vM/\" target=\"\">Shawnee wraps up baseball investigation, students face ‘consequences’ </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nParker Perry</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate is-truncated\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>The Clark-Shawnee Local School District has completed its internal investigation into the school’s baseball team and said students will face consequences. endangering after reportedly leaving her nephews home alone. The children were located by witnesses and police three blocks from their home in the area of&nbsp;South Center Street and West Southern Avenue around 10:30 a.m. April 20. The victims are a 1-year-old boy and 2-year-old boy, according to a Springfield police report. Ty'eisha Moore, 25, is facing two counts of endangering children. She was booked into Clark County Jail.&nbsp; The two children had been located without adult supervision, police said. Witnesses spent 15 minutes trying to locate a parent or guardian.&nbsp; The children&rsquo;s mother told police she had left the children in the care of her sister while she stayed with a loved one admitted to an area hospital.&nbsp; Moore lives with her sister in the 300 block of West Southern Avenue in Springfield. Mark your calendars and keep checking in for the latest forecast. Lyrids: Active April 16-25. Rates are usually 10-20 meteors per hour. The moon will be a waning crescent so the sky will be pretty dark. This meteor shower is associated with fireballs which are very bright. The shower peaks pre-dawn April 22.\nEta Aquariids: Active April 19 to May 26. Rates are usually 10-30 meteors per hour. The moon is a waxing gibbous so it will shine bright in the sky. The shower peaks before dawn May 7.\nAlpha Capicornids: Active July 11 to August 10. Only produces about five meteors per hour but is known to produce fireballs. The shower peaks July 26-27.\nDelta Aquariids: Active July 21 to August 23. This shower is best in the southern hemisphere. There is usually a good number meteors the week surrounding the peak which is July 30.\nPerseids: Active July 13 to August 26. This is an active shower that produces 50-70 meteors per hour. The peak night is August 11-12. The moon will be near full and might be bright.\nSouthern Taurids: Active September 7 to November 19. It is long but doesn&rsquo;t have an impressive peak. You could see an increased chance for fireball sightings. The shower peaks October 9-10.\nOrionids: Active October 4 to November 14. A typical year it can produce 20-25 meteors per hour. The shower peaks October 21-22.\nNorthern Taurids: Active October 19 to December 10. Can be active the same time as the Southern Taurids. The shower peaks November 10-11.\nLeonids: Active November 5-30. The rates are usually about 15 meteors per hours but there can be outbursts some years. The shower peaks November 17-18.\nGeminids: Active December 4-16. This is a great meteor shower during the year. They can have long tails and bright colors. The showers will peak December 13-14.\nUrsids: Active December 17-23. The shower usually produces five to 10 meteors per hour but an outburst can take the rate up to 25 meteors per hour. Wednesday:&nbsp;A frontal boundary pushing across the Miami Valley will bring a chance for a passing shower or thunderstorm. Highs will be in the middle 70s. Thursday: &nbsp;Temperatures will be unseasonably warm under partly cloudy skies.   \n<!-- map content:  The Clark County Historical Society will host an American Civil War themed symposium this weekend featuring period music, artifacts and lectures from noted historians. The seventh annual Civil War Symposium will focus on topics such as the homefront and the Reconstruction era after the war on Saturday at the Heritage Center of Clark County, 117 S. Fountain Ave. The event, which starts at 9 a.m., will be held at the center&rsquo;s rotating gallery room and will include a continental breakfast, a catered lunch and refreshments. RELATED: Stafford: News-Sun morgue alive at Heritage Center Four noted historians from across the country will each present a lecture on a different aspect of the war. The presentations will begin with Professor Thomas Taylor from Wittenberg University reviewing the contributions of Springfield native J. Warren Kiefer, an accomplished general and member of the U.S. House of Representatives during the mid to late 19th century. Former West Point Professor Ethan Rafuse will explore the role of Stonewall Jackson in the conflict. The lecture series will continue with Professor Mark Grimsley from Ohio State University discussing Reconstruction. Professor Nicole Etcheson of Ball State University will talk about the effects that the war had on families on the homefront. It&rsquo;s the first year that the home front will be examined during the symposium. Historical Civil War period music will also be featured and will include performances from local musician Steven Ball. Artifacts from the Civil War will be on display and will include pieces from the private collections of Clark County residents. DETAILS: John Legend one of TIME 100 most influential people of 2017 Attendees are also encouraged to check out the Civil War exhibit at the Heritage Center. The symposium began in 2010 as part of the 150th anniversary of the American Civil War. Clark County resident Ski Schanner, a local history buff, believed it would be a good idea to host an event for it. &ldquo;Across the country, especially east of the Mississippi, communities were beginning to do that for a five-year period of time to commemorate the sesquicentennial,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;I thought we would do the same thing in Springfield.&rdquo; Schanner worked with the Clark County Historical Society to recruit lecturers from across the country and since then they have thrown an annual Civil War Symposium, each year dealing with new aspects of the Civil War. Tickets for the symposium will be $60 for adults, $50 for members and $20 for students. The historical society will offer a scholarship for students from seventh to 12th grades to attend. For more information on the symposium, tickets and scholarship opportunities contact the Clark County Historical Society at 937-324-0657 -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/local-education/civil-war-symposium-returns-clark-county/V0J6ofcWi9xRWmKvF8ipjI/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/SpringfieldNewsSun/2017/04/21/Images/newsEngin.18439033_IMG_9563.jpg\" title=\"Civil War Symposium returns to Clark County \" alt=\"Civil War Symposium returns to Clark County \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/local-education/civil-war-symposium-returns-clark-county/V0J6ofcWi9xRWmKvF8ipjI/\" target=\"\">Civil War Symposium returns to Clark County </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nHasan Karim, <span class=\"story-source\">Staff Writer</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>The Clark County Historical Society will host an American Civil War themed symposium this weekend featuring period music, artifacts and lectures from noted historians. money for projects at two Ohio Air National Guard bases. U.S. Rep. Mike Turner and state Reps. Niraj Antani and Rick Perales spoke at a Thursday press conference about their frustrations with Wright-Patterson&rsquo;s being shut out in a plea for funding for four infrastructure projects, covering everything from roof repairs to security gates. Antani, R-Miami Twp., and Perales, R-Beavercreek, vowed to work to change how the money was awarded and review who sits on the nine-member Ohio Military Facilities Commission, five of whom were former high-ranking Ohio National Guard leaders. Turner, R-Dayton, said he was &ldquo;outraged&rdquo; when he learned the commission bypassed Wright-Patterson for a share of millions of dollars to help pay for infrastructure projects. &ldquo;We know there are facilities that need funding all across the state but certainly with the largest single-site employer in the state right here in the Miami Valley we were very surprised and disappointed that not a dollar of the $5 million would come to Dayton, Ohio,&rdquo; he said at a press conference in his downtown Dayton district office. The congressman, a member of the U.S. House Armed Services Committee, said he had worked with Ohio House Speaker Cliff Rosenberger, R-Clarksville, to push for the creation of a pot of state money to defray infrastructure costs at federal installations like Wright-Patterson in a bid to compete with other states doing the same thing and out to capture new missions and jobs. Turner said he expects a base realignment and closure process, or BRAC, will begin next year and start to have an impact in 2020. Wright-Patterson has a workforce of about 27,000, the most at one employer site in Ohio. The panel voted to send $2.5 million to help relocate a taxiway at Mansfield Lahm Air National Guard Base and $2.5 million to help build a combined deployment and processing and physical fitness center at Toledo Air National Guard Base. &ldquo;As the largest single-site employer, the entire region benefits from Wright-Patterson Air Force Base and if this is only going to fund athletic facilities at National Gaurd bases which really don&rsquo;t face a threat from the federal government to move jobs and missions around, we don&rsquo;t want to support that funding,&rdquo; Antani said. For the panel to divide the &ldquo;entire pot of funds for two facilities makes you question really what was doing on with the commission and the implications that were in front of them,&rdquo; Turner said. The evaluation process should include the workforce size at the facility and the spin-off technology created to &ldquo;reflect the true value of the military installations&rdquo; in Ohio, Perales said. He has a budget request for $5 million more to fund the program in the future. The two state lawmakers also noted the majority, or five of the nine members of the commission, were former high-ranking Ohio National Guard leaders. Antani said the Dayton region, which had one commission member, should have a higher share of the appointees with the state&rsquo;s largest work site at Wright-Patterson. &ldquo;I think we need to retake a look at who&rsquo;s getting appointed and if they have a conflict of interest in serving in the National Guard,&rdquo; he said in part. In an email Thursday, Commission Chairman Mark D. Wagoner Jr., a Toledo area lawyer and former state lawmaker, referred questions about who sits on the commission to the appointing authorities, which were the House, Senate and the governor&rsquo;s office. &ldquo;I would also note that the vote of the Commission was unanimous, so the four members with no previous affiliation with the National Guard also voted to adopt the rankings of the neutral evaluator,&rdquo; he wore in an email. According to an OFMC document, chosen projects had applications with supporting documents that gave &ldquo;much greater detail directly tying the infrastructure projects to military value&rdquo; and the last base realignment and closure guidelines. Pike County is April 22.&nbsp; Despite intense media scrutiny and around-the-clock work by investigators, there still remain more questions than answers.&nbsp; Here are five mysteries that still remain one year later: &#65279;At various times in the investigation there have been differing theories on who killed the Rhoden family.&nbsp; After a previous drug bust in the area was mentioned, statements by Ohio Attorney General Mike DeWine led to speculation that a Mexican drug cartel could have been involved. Commercial marijuana grow operations were found at two of the four murder scenes.&nbsp; But DeWine and Pike County Sheriff Charles Reader have since expressed the belief that the perpetrators &ndash; the consensus is that there&rsquo;s more than one &ndash; were local to Pike County. Some officials have speculated the suspects were even closer to the family. Leonard Manley, whose daughter Dana Rhoden, 37, was one of the eight victims, has previously said whoever entered the property had to be familiar to the family&rsquo;s dogs, or else they would have barked and attacked.&nbsp; The victims suffered a combined 32 gunshot wounds &ndash; one was shot nine times, two were shot five times each &ndash; and some showed soft tissue bruising, suggesting they may have been beaten, according to autopsy reports. DeWine and Reader would not say at a recent press conference if they believe they&rsquo;ve interviewed the perpetrator(s).&nbsp; DeWine and Reader gave a press conference April 13 and stressed that the case is still a top priority.&nbsp; But after more than 800 tips, 400 interviews, 38 search warrants and a dozen investigators constantly on the ground in Piketon, there have been no arrests.&nbsp; DeWine and Reader declined to discuss possible motives, a time-frame for the investigation or scenarios that have been ruled out.&nbsp; They insist a lack of disclosure about new details will protect the integrity of the investigation &mdash; the largest in the Ohio Bureau of Criminal Investigation history. They also insist the case is not cold, and that there exists &mdash; somewhere in the county, or elsewhere &mdash; someone with answers. &ldquo;We will find you,&rdquo; Reader said to the killer or killers. &ldquo;We will arrest you, and you will be prosecuted.&rdquo; The sheriff&rsquo;s office has hung posters all in various locations around Piketon, advertising a $10,000 reward for information that leads to an arrest in the case.&nbsp; Crime Stoppers is accepting donations to increase that amount.&nbsp; But Reader admitted at the recent press conference that fear of reprisal is still a real factor in this case.&nbsp; He said he&rsquo;s sure some of the people they&rsquo;ve interviewed have lied to them, whether out of fear of getting in trouble for criminal activity, or out of fear that they could be next.&nbsp; The killings were committed&nbsp;&ldquo;execution-style,&rdquo; and whoever did it is still out there. In the immediate aftermath of the crimes, Reader told other members of the Rhoden family to arm themselves for protection.&nbsp; DeWine said solving the murders is the top priority, and detectives need information. &ldquo;This is a homicide investigation,&rdquo; he said.&nbsp;&ldquo;It&rsquo;s not that we don&rsquo;t care what you&rsquo;ve done with drugs &ndash; we care, but our focus is on the homicide, and so people should not be concerned about coming forward and disclosing information that may be helpful in the investigation.&rdquo; Was the family killed by rival drug dealers or were the murders in connection to other criminal activity? There was evidence they were raising roosters for cock fighting, DeWine has said. This criminal activity, Reader said, was &ldquo;minute&rdquo; compared to their slaughter. &ldquo;Regardless of their lifestyle, they were human beings,&rdquo; he said.&nbsp; Was the murder more personal? In the immediate aftermath, several individuals who had expressed prior disputes&nbsp;with Rhoden family members on social media were questioned, but quickly let go.&nbsp; There have been more rumors than concrete theories when it comes to motive.&nbsp; Things are quieter now in the rural community, but the specter of these slayings, unsolved, still hangs over the people of Pike County. A billboard with the victim&rsquo;s faces literally hovers over the sheriff&rsquo;s office.&nbsp; &ldquo;I see the look of disappointment when I speak with the family, and I look into their eyes and the grieving they still have,&rdquo; Reader said last week. He said these murders were the worst thing he&rsquo;s seen in all his years in law enforcement.&nbsp; &ldquo;There will always be a scar on this town,&rdquo; said&nbsp;Morty Throckmorton, who manages the Smart Mart store in Piketon. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/pike-county-murders-unanswered-questions-year-later/f5PnS126ILLe1O43mvjrCN/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image/Pub/p8/WHIO/2017/04/19/Videos/4032325.vpx\" title=\"Pike County murders: 5 unanswered questions 1 year later \" alt=\"Pike County murders: 5 unanswered questions 1 year later \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/pike-county-murders-unanswered-questions-year-later/f5PnS126ILLe1O43mvjrCN/\" target=\"\">Pike County murders: 5 unanswered questions 1 year later </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t  \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\nKatie Wedell</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>The one-year anniversary of the brutal slayings of eight family members in rural Pike County is April 22. The Storm Prediction Center has said the main threat is for damaging wind gusts up to 70 mph with isolated hail up to 1.5 inches in diameter. The next round of storms is expected to arrive around 8 p.m., Storm Center 7 Chief Meteorologist Eric Elwell said. Your Storm Center 7 team still agrees that an isolated severe storm or two will be possible as we head towards early evening.&nbsp; We will likely see storms first fire north of Interstate 70 then push southeast through the evening.&nbsp; The greatest threat from these storms will be from strong winds and large hail, along with locally heavy rain and lightning. Driving in the evening hours may become problematic with the threat for severe storms and heavy downpours reducing visibility and creating ponding on the roads. If you don't already have it, make sure to download our WHIO Weather App to get the latest alerts. You can also listen to AM 1290 and News 95.7 WHIO if you are in the car later today or watch our 90 minutes of news this evening as your Storm Center 7 Team tracks the latest.&nbsp; -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/local/threat-showers-storms-tonight/x4CDYaqbK3jE7qOr9fOyVN/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image/Pub/p8/WHIO/2017/04/20/Videos/4039738.vpx\" title=\"Threat of showers, storms tonight \" alt=\"Threat of showers, storms tonight \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/local/threat-showers-storms-tonight/x4CDYaqbK3jE7qOr9fOyVN/\" target=\"\">Threat of showers, storms tonight </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t  \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\nBreaking News Staff</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>After a dry and warm start to the week, the threat for storms returns to the Miami Valley. DeVos and American Federation of Teachers President Randi Weingarten are meeting with pre-K, early childhood and special education teachers and staff.   \n            \n            \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  Trustees for the State Teachers Retirement System of Ohio voted 10-1 on Thursday to indefinitely suspend the cost of living allowance given to retired teachers. Retirees will no longer get a 2 percent COLA bump on their pensions for the foreseeable future. The fix, though, may not be enough to shore up the finances of the $72-billion fund. &ldquo;Even if we take this action, it&rsquo;s only a 50-50 shot that it works,&rdquo; said STRS trustee James McGreevy, who argued for broader, deeper changes. McGreevy was the only no vote. Actuaries calculate that the pension fund has a 33 percent change of hitting all its targets &mdash; investment returns, mortality, retirement rates and other assumptions &mdash; every year in the next six years. Depending on how far off target STRS is, more changes could be required. Likewise, if investment returns exceed expectations, trustees could decide to revisit the COLA issue. The move, which impacts 490,000 teachers and retirees statewide, comes after consultants told STRS Ohio to dial back its expected annual rate of return on its $72.1 billion investment portfolio. The rate had been set at 7.75 percent &mdash; too rosy. Likewise, consultants and actuaries advised trustees that the system&rsquo;s assumptions about payroll growth and life expectancy for teachers and retirees were out of whack. Earlier this year, trustees agreed to change assumptions but in doing so, accrued liabilities ballooned on the financial sheets. Ohio pension systems are required to be able to pay off their unfunded liabilities within a 30-year window. But with the assumption changes, STRS was looking at a 57.7-year window. Retired Beavercreek teacher Linda Beaver, of Miamisburg, said losing the COLA means &ldquo;you do more with less.&rdquo; &ldquo;Retirees wil have to decide what is most important and set priorities. Probably less travel, less eating out, just have to make the money go farther,&rdquo; Beaver said. STRS trustees agreed that the cost of living allowance issue will be reviewed within five years. Beaver said she would have preferred that the board look at it annually. &ldquo;Five years is a long time to wait for a review.&rdquo; Dean Dennis, a retired Cincinnati teacher, blasted the trustees to listening to public comments &mdash; after the vote was taken. &ldquo;I paid into that system for 35 years. My employer paid into it for 35 years. They met all their earnings assumptions. They set that money aside for me and now they&rsquo;re taking it out,&rdquo; Dennis said. &ldquo;They&rsquo;re just robbing my pension.&rdquo; Like in other states, Ohio&rsquo;s public pensions are defined benefits systems. The pension benefit is based on age, years of service and final average salary and it&rsquo;s guaranteed. Defined contribution plans, such as 401(k) funds, are more common in the private sector. Public employees in Ohio do not participate in Social Security. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/retired-ohio-teachers-lose-cost-living-increase/AkcWxMTYfJUg09C5xAcguL/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image/Pub/p8/WHIO/2017/04/20/Videos/4039656.vpx\" title=\"Retired Ohio teachers to lose cost of living increase \" alt=\"Retired Ohio teachers to lose cost of living increase \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/retired-ohio-teachers-lose-cost-living-increase/AkcWxMTYfJUg09C5xAcguL/\" target=\"\">Retired Ohio teachers to lose cost of living increase </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t  \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\nLaura A. Conference football. But instead of continuing a successful run at Triad, Payton Printz will be hoping to do the same at Southeastern High School. Printz recently was approved by the Southeastern Local School District to be its football coach. He succeeds Greg Bonifay, who resigned earlier this spring to accept a similar position at Greater Western Ohio Conference member Stebbins. Printz took a perennially losing program at Triad and transformed the Cardinals into an OHC power during a 15-year run as coach at the North Lewisburg program. Last season he was an assistant at Minster, which overcame a 2-4 record to play in the Division VII state championship. Southeastern was 3-7 last season, the most wins since the Trojans were 5-5 in 2012. Southeastern is a Division VII, Region 28 program. Here&rsquo;s what Printz had to say about all that and more: Q: You seemed to have it all at Minster, a job, success and you&rsquo;re in the Midwest Athletic Conference. Why return to the OHC to a rival program? A: It became apparent to me I needed to be a head coach. I need to be the one in charge. Don&rsquo;t get me wrong; it was one of the greatest experiences I&rsquo;ve ever had to be involved with that staff under Geron Stokes at Minster. The trek to the state championship game was memorable. Just a whirlwind. Q: You&rsquo;ve experienced small-ball football in two conferences. What&rsquo;s the difference? A: People have asked me for years, what&rsquo;s the difference between the MAC and the OHC? It&rsquo;s a different breed of (MAC) kid. It seemed very obvious to me. A lot of it is genetics. These kids are just huge, a lot of them. They come from great two-parent families. They have a Catholic upbringing and there&rsquo;s no youth sports there to speak of. No soccer. When we played Marion Local, 19 of their kids walked by me who were bigger than me. I thought, my goodness, what have we got ourselves into? It was unbelievable. I&rsquo;d never seen that many big kids up close on the same high school football team. Minster has 97 boys and 72 are on the (football) roster. They&rsquo;re in the weight room 6 a.m. to 7:30 a.m. three days a week and they&rsquo;re never late. They never miss. It&rsquo;s a different mentality. Q: Why Southeastern? A: That&rsquo;s the main reason I&rsquo;m doing it; it&rsquo;s a great challenge. When I got to Triad it was the same situation it&rsquo;s going to be at South Charleston. I surrounded myself with good people and I&rsquo;m going to do the same thing. I&rsquo;m going to try and build a staff with somebody who buys into what we&rsquo;re about and what we&rsquo;re trying to do. I&rsquo;m going to try and change the culture. Q: What can Southeastern players expect? A: These kids are not going to expect some of the things that&rsquo;s coming at them. We&rsquo;re going to work extremely hard and the cream is going to rise to the top. We had a similar philosophy at Triad. You&rsquo;re going to put in the work and if (you do that) you&rsquo;ll benefit from it and it&rsquo;ll pay dividends down the road for you. We did things at Triad that nobody in their right mind should ever have done. That&rsquo;s a school that predominantly had never won. We went to the playoffs five times and six other times we were either ninth or 10th in the region and just missed qualifying. It was an exceptional run for those young men and we hope to do some of the similar things at Southeastern. Q: How do you do that? A: Southeastern has great athletes. You see it in basketball. You&rsquo;ve got to get some of those kids out (for football). You have to get the good athletes throughout the building and get them out playing football. If we can do that we&rsquo;ll be competitive again. We have five D-V schools, three D-VI and two D-VII teams on the schedule. If you win the right games, we could turn into some playoff-bound teams. It&rsquo;s not going to happen overnight. It&rsquo;s a challenge from top to bottom to get ready and it&rsquo;s exciting. I can&rsquo;t wait. Dunbar High School football coach. His status as coach had been under scrutiny since a series of events last season ultimately led to one of the harshest penalties in Ohio High School Athletic Association history. Powell, a Dunbar graduate, told his returning players on Thursday his coaching contract for the upcoming season had been renewed. &ldquo;It was tears,&rdquo; said Powell during Friday night&rsquo;s Dayton Edwin C. Moses Relays at Welcome Stadium. &ldquo;Everybody was happy. We always stuck together and we stood up to everything. Now, we&rsquo;re just moving forward.&rdquo; Powell and the Wolverines football coaching staff and boys basketball coach Pete Pullen, formerly the school&rsquo;s athletic director, were central figures in a standoff with Dayton Public Schools director of athletics Mark Baker. The coaching staff accused Baker of suggesting Dunbar should lose to Belmont in a Week 10 game last fall at Welcome Stadium. Had that happened, it was presumed both City League teams would have qualified for the playoffs, which would have been a first for DPS football. Baker denied the allegation. Two bizarre offensive plays by Dunbar early in the third quarter appeared to confirm the Wolverines had been instructed to &ldquo;throw the game,&rdquo; according to an OHSAA finding. A shortened video of the DPS-produced game tape of those two plays was an instant Internet hit last week. Ultimately, an academically ineligible player resulted in Dunbar having to forfeit Week 9-10 games, missing the playoffs, bumping other teams out of the playoffs and readjusting several first-round playoff matchups in multiple divisions. Just two weeks ago the OHSAA announced its ruling, citing a &ldquo;serious lack of administrative responsibility and institutional control.&rdquo; All DPS boys and girls athletic teams were immediately placed on a three-year probation and the district was fined $10,000. It was believed to be the first time an entire school district was penalized by the OHSAA. A separate investigation by DPS concluded that Baker did not instruct Dunbar to lose to Belmont, according to a statement by superintendent Rhonda Corr. Powell&rsquo;s status as returning coach was in doubt because, according to multiple coaches, a new question had been added to the interview process, inquiring about any DPS reprimand. Both Powell and Pullen were reprimanded for allowing the academically ineligible player to play against Belmont. DPS would not confirm that was a new interview question. Pullen resigned as the Dunbar AD in mid-November. &ldquo;This is what I wanted,&rdquo; said Powell. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m glad to be back and I&rsquo;m glad everything is over with so we can move forward. Powell also said Dunbar was not hit with an exodus of football players who might have transferred had the OHSAA also ruled the Wolverines were not allowed to participate in the postseason. Dunbar was 9-1 on the field last season and 7-3 after the forfeits. After a hot start, Cincinnati and its pitchers are cooling off. Kind of like our recent weather pattern. You don&rsquo;t see many grand slams in baseball, and even fewer triple plays (only 19 in the big leagues since 2010, according to baseballreference.com). Fairmont High School checked both of those rare boxes during a home victory last Tuesday against Springfield. First, Firebirds junior Kenny Sherick hit a grand slam in the bottom of the fourth inning. Not to be outdone, the Fairmont defense stepped up with a 5-4-3 triple play in the top of the fifth. My scout at the game reported that third baseman Chaney Morgan started it by fielding a ground ball and stepping on third base. He fired to Trevor Cukovecki, who made the turn to Alex Stefanidis. That is some extremely impressive baseball &mdash; at any level. Some UD fans are wringing their hands over the loss of point guard John Crosby, who was granted a release from his scholarship. The angst shows how low the bar has dropped for some of the faithful. I&rsquo;m guessing new coach Anthony Grant did not hesitate to grant Crosby&rsquo;s request. In fact, this might have been a case of Crosby seeing the writing on the wall. This is addition by subtraction, folks. Somebody will be dribbling the basketball upcourt for the Flyers next season. There is quality playing time available, a great recruiting pitch as Grant tries to sell Dayton to a competent grad transfer, a JUCO standout, or a certain prep standout from Minnesota. It&rsquo;s great the First Four will stay at UD Arena through 2022. That&rsquo;s a win-win for UD and the city. But one question that needs to be asked after the NCAA&rsquo;s announcement of upcoming tourney sites: Will Dayton ever again be a host for first- and second-round games? Those basketball-packed days are the jewels of the tournament, and UD has not hosted those rounds since 2013. Heck, U.S. Bank Arena in downtown Cincinnati, a real dump, was named as a 2022 site &ndash; contingent on a pricey renovation. If that doesn&rsquo;t materialize, perhaps UD Arena will be Plan B. I think the arena&rsquo;s low seating capacity works against it. Luke Fickell got his first big win at the University of Cincinnati last week. UC&rsquo;s new football coach got a commitment from Malik Vann, a 4-star defensive end from nearby Fairfield. Vann had offers from a bunch of powerhouse schools including Alabama, Florida State and Notre Dame. That is some big-time recruiting. Trending up: Eric Thames, Mike Leake, Anthony Rizzo. 4,000 pounds of chicken soup due to mislabeling. &ldquo;Campbell&rsquo;s Homestyle Healthy Request Chicken with Whole Grain Pasta&rdquo; 18.6 oz. cans with a best buy date of Feb. 13, 2019,&nbsp;have been recalled. The cans were filled with the wrong soup; Campbell&rsquo;s Homestyle Healthy Request Italian-Style Wedding Spinach & Meatballs in Chicken Broth, which contains milk.    \n    \n        \n        \n        \n        \n            \n            \n            \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  The Cincinnati Reds trolled Chicago Cubs fans with the&nbsp;Bandwagon Cam on Friday. The camera found a fan who had never been to Chicago (allegedly), one who didn&rsquo;t know who Ernie Banks was (maybe) and another who bought his Cubs shirt that day (probably). Cubs fans had a good laugh about it, maybe because they figured they would get the last laugh &mdash; and they did with a 6-5, 11-inning victory. A day later, the Cubs enjoyed the first laugh, the last laugh and most of the laughs in between at Great American Ball Park in front of a crowd of 27,189 fans, the majority of them wearing Chicago blue. The Cubs beat the Reds 12-8 Saturday to ensure they will win their seventh series in the last eight against Cincinnati. The Cubs won this game with three swings of the bat. Those swings produced 10 runs. Two of those swings came against Reds starter Cody Reed, who allowed seven earned runs in two innings. Reed didn&rsquo;t have much to say after the game. This was his chance to prove he belongs in the starting rotation after four scoreless appearances in relief, and if Tim Adleman continues pitching well and Rookie Davis returns from injury, Reed could find himself right back in the bullpen. &ldquo;I wasn&rsquo;t that good,&rdquo; Reed said. &ldquo;&lsquo;I&rsquo;ll just keep on keeping on, pretty much.&rdquo; The Reds (9-9) lost their fourth straight game. They are 2-7 on this homestand with one game left Sunday. The Cubs (10-7) lead the Reds by 1&frac12; games in the division. Reed gave up a three-run home run by Anthony Rizzo in the first and a grand slam to Wilson Contreras in the second. Wildness plagued him. He walked five batters. His day ended after two innings and 69 pitches. Lisalverto Bonilla, called up to the Reds from Triple-A Louisville on Tuesday, took over for Reed and made his Reds debut. He allowed four earned runs on three hits in five innings. A three-run home run by Jason Heyward in the sixth put the game out of reach. The Reds offense did its part. Joey Votto hit a three-run home run and drove in two more runs. Eugenio Suarez hit two home runs. The Reds couldn&rsquo;t overcome Reed&rsquo;s poor performance. He looked like the pitcher who was 0-7 with a 7.36 ERA in 10 starts as a rookie in 2016, not the pitcher coming off six straight perfect innings in relief. &ldquo;The talent is in there,&rdquo; Reds manager Bryan Price said. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s part our responsibility but also his responsibility to extract that talent and let it work. It&rsquo;s going to sort itself out. I think he&rsquo;s a big-league pitcher. If it&rsquo;s as a starter or as a reliever, that&rsquo;s undefined, and we don&rsquo;t have to define him at his age, but we&rsquo;ve got to find a way to put us in a spot where he can help our ballclub. &ldquo;We can&rsquo;t use every season as a trial-and-error season. We do have to take steps forward. I think we have to start the season, but we can&rsquo;t use every year that way where we bring up young pitchers and introduce them and put up with the growing pains. We have to take bigger steps with the younger pitching than we have to this point.&rdquo; -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/sports/chicago-cubs-hand-cincinnati-reds-fourth-straight-loss/ZYi7kq99xilu7hBSne1pAJ/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image/Pub/p8/WHIO/2017/04/22/Videos/4043215.vpx\" title=\"Chicago Cubs hand Cincinnati Reds fourth straight loss \" alt=\"Chicago Cubs hand Cincinnati Reds fourth straight loss \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/sports/chicago-cubs-hand-cincinnati-reds-fourth-straight-loss/ZYi7kq99xilu7hBSne1pAJ/\" target=\"\">Chicago Cubs hand Cincinnati Reds fourth straight loss </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t  \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\nDavid Jablonski</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>The Cincinnati Reds trolled Chicago Cubs fans with the Bandwagon Cam on Friday. Bumgarner pitched in Wednesday&rsquo;s 2-0 loss at Kansas City, allowing one run in six innings. Giants manager said that Bumgarner was &ldquo;very remorseful.&rdquo; &ldquo;Here's a young guy, like a lot of us who think we're invincible, he's just having fun that day, hit a slippery spot and went down,&rdquo; Bochy told ESPN. &ldquo;I'm sure, looking at it now, Madison wishes he wouldn't have gotten on [the bike]. &hellip; Unfortunately, an accident happened.&rdquo; -->\n<article \n<!-- map content:  Despite having watched his Cincinnati Reds lose 18 of their last 22 games against the Chicago Cubs, manager Bryan Price was looking forward on Friday to the season&rsquo;s first matchup with the &mdash; still-weird-to-say &mdash; defending World Series champions. &ldquo;Yeah, we&rsquo;re facing the champs,&rdquo; he said before the game. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s been a big couple of years for the Cubs. They&rsquo;ve put together a really nice team. There are a lot of teams out there that are trying to get there, and we&rsquo;re trying to do the same thing. This is a good way of measuring ourselves.&rdquo; The first test was promising &mdash; until the ninth, when Anthony Rizzo followed a Miguel Montero single and Kyle Schwarber double by slamming the first pitch he saw from Michael Lorenzen into the first row of the right-field seats for a three-run home run and a 5-5 tie. The Cubs completed the rally in the 11th against Robert Stephenson on Albert Almora&rsquo;s one-out walk, Schwarber&rsquo;s single to right and Kris Bryant&rsquo;s sacrifice fly for a 6-5 win that knocked the Reds out of first place for the first time in 17 days. Lorenzen&rsquo;s plan for Rizzo was simple. &ldquo;Challenge him,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;I was going to throw every pitch with conviction. I threw with conviction, he swung with conviction. He got me. He&rsquo;s a good hitter. He&rsquo;s Anthony Rizzo for a reason. Stuff like this happens. What separates you is how you handle it. What sucks is that the team played so well. You feel like you let your teammates down.&rdquo; Price knew he&rsquo;d opened himself up for criticism by leaving the right-handed Lorenzen in to face the left-handed Rizzo with left-hander Wandy Peralta warming up. &ldquo;Wandy would have been in there if they had taken the lead,&rdquo; Price said. &ldquo;We had the right matchup. It just didn&rsquo;t work out. It&rsquo;s a second-guesser&rsquo;s delight. We were one pitch away. We just didn&rsquo;t get it done. I&rsquo;m not going to lose any sleep over the decisions. We had the best guy available in there. He was the best guy in that situation.&rdquo; For the third straight game, a strong start by a Reds pitcher was wasted. Tim Adleman set a career-high with seven strikeouts and added a tie-breaking, two-run double, but the Reds slipped to 2-6 on their 10-game home stand. In the previous two games, good-enough-to-win efforts by Amir Garrett and Scott Feldman went unrewarded by a Reds offense that put together a scoreless-inning streak that eventually reached 26 innings. Playing most of the game in a steady rain before a crowd of 29,350 bolstered by the usual influx of visiting Cubs fans, the Reds left the bases loaded twice. Adleman, making his first start after being recalled from Triple-A Louisville on Sunday and pitching four innings of relief after rookie Sal Romano lasted three in his major-league debut, allowed four hits and two runs &ndash; home runs by Jason Heyward and Javier Baez &ndash; with two walks in six innings. &ldquo;It was a little spotty at times, but if you had told me six innings and two runs against the Cubs before the game, I would have taken it,&rdquo; Adleman said. Right-hander Drew Storen allowed the first two runners to reach in the seventh before bouncing back with a strikeout and a flyout, then coaxing Bryant, the reigning National League MVP, into popping out foul to catcher Tucker Barnhart. Joey Votto, who went into the game hitting .350 (7-for-20) with four runs batted in over his last six games, batted twice with the bases loaded and two outs and both times flied out to center field, the second shot a hard line drive in the sixth. Heyward drove his first home run of the season into the right field seats with two outs in the fourth inning to give the Cubs a 1-0 lead. The Reds, whose four hits in the first three innings matched their total from the previous two games, got a spark from their two most effective recent hitters to rally for a 3-1 lead in the bottom of the inning. Eugenio Suarez lined a double up the right-center field gap with one out, and Zack Cozart followed with a bloop RBI double down the left field line. After Scott Schebler grounded out to Lester, Barnhart was intentionally walked and Adleman made the Cubs pay with an improbable drive into the left-center field gap for a two-run double. Javier Baez led off the Chicago fifth with his first homer of the season, but Duvall &ndash; 2-for-25 on the home stand going into the at bat &ndash; got the run back with an opposite-field homer to right in the bottom of the fifth. The Reds capitalized on a bout of wildness to add a run with a two-out rally in the sixth. \n<!-- map content:  Mike Defibaugh is living his dream. The 43-year-old Bellefontaine native and resident not only fished in his first professional walleye tournament April 12-13, but he won it. And with that, he announced he would be turning over all $15,000 of his cash winnings to the Make-A-Wish Foundation, which grants requests to children diagnosed with life-threatening diseases. &ldquo;To be fishing as a pro on the tournament circuit is a dream come true for me,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;My wife (Mandy) and I talked about it when we decided I should turn pro and we decided we would donate any cash I earned this year. And since I am living my dream, we figured we would help kids have a chance to live their dreams.&rdquo; The tournament that he won was the Cabela&rsquo;s National Walleye Tour season-opening event out of Huron on Lake Erie. In addition to his $15,000 cash, first prize included a new $46,000 Ranger boat and Evinrude motor. Defibaugh&rsquo;s knowledge of Lake Erie paid off on the second and final day of the tournament. As the boats headed out of the Huron River, almost all turned left to find some of the large spawning walleyes off of the Camp Perry reefs &mdash; quite a long haul from Huron. Instead, Defibaugh turned right and fished close to shore, 5-8 miles east of Huron. &ldquo;The lake was muddy and the wind was blowing,&rdquo; Defibaugh recalled. &ldquo;So I looked for places with better water clarity. I think I ended up just five miles from the ramp.&rdquo; Defibaugh was using deep-diving Bandits with planer boards. His color combinations varied, but a chartreuse head, purple sides and a pink belly worked best. He said the more important adjustment was adding Pro-Cure, a gel scent, on the top of each crankbait. &ldquo;I think the scent was key to locating the crankbaits,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;They were hitting it hard; the boards would just rocket back. There was no mistaking there was a fish on.&rdquo; He finished with 64.65 pounds, 42.26 coming on the second day when he rose from 22nd place after the first day with 22.23 pounds. &ldquo;I went west to the reefs the first day and caught one good fish (out of the five he weighed). With the way the wind was blowing on the second day, I decided to stay closer to home.&rdquo; The second-place finisher was Ryan Buddie of North Ridgeville, Ohio at 62.61 pounds. Mike Robertson of Laura came in fourth with 58.53 pounds and won $16,568. Defibaugh, who is a mechanic for CSX Railroad, credits his wife and sponsors (Ranger, Knox Marine, Indian Lake Bass Pro and Reef Runner Lures) for giving him his chance on the pro circuit. &ldquo;I am lucky my wife and kids (three) have been behind me all the way. We talked about turning pro all winter,&rdquo; said Defibaugh. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve been blessed with a job that keeps all the bills paid with a little left over to put gas in the boat and buy a few worms. State Route 560, Urbana Set of 8.36 acres, property includes barn Contributing writer Situated on 8.36 acres, this remodeled farmhouse at 576 N. State Route 560 in Urbana boasts a working orchard with 28 varieties of apple trees. The property is listed by Henderson Land Investment Co. for $285,000. The apple orchard was open for business until November 2016. The property includes a 75-by-40-foot barn that was rehabbed in 1993 and converted to a pole barn. A water line runs from the house to the barn. Special features of this distinctive home include cathedral ceilings, French doors, hardwood floors, walk-in closets, a formal dining room, a sunny breakfast room, wood-burning stove and an open, spiral staircase. Built in 1900, the home has three bedrooms, two full bathrooms and approximately 2,720 square feet of living space. The house has a partial crawl space foundation and a partial, unfinished basement. The exterior of the home is vinyl. Exterior features include a detached, two-car garage, a covered front porch and a long gravel driveway that ends with a parking area in front of the barn. The detached garage is heated with a propane heater and has a floored second story. A 16-by-20-foot, screened porch with a brick floor overlooks the back and side yards. This home has seen many updates, including new roofs on both the house and the garage that were added in 2014 and a new septic system that was installed in 2013. The detached garage and an addition to the home were added in 1990. Sheltered by the covered front porch, the front door opens to an entry space with a hallway that leads to the main living area of the home. An open concept living room and sunken family room offer space for entertaining. Features include neutral carpeting, a spiral staircase that leads to the second floor, a wood-burning stove and oversized windows with half round windows at the top. The kitchen features a breakfast bar center island plus an abundance of wood cabinetry and counter space. The dishwasher, range and refrigerator are included in the price of the home. Just off the kitchen is the formal dining room as well as a breakfast room that is lined with long windows that offer views of the apple orchard. Children in the neighborhood attend school in the Graham Local school district. \n<!-- map content:  Earlier this month, Anna Gros of Fairborn was driving home when she turned onto Interstate 675 and hit a large piece of concrete sticking out of a pothole. The large piece of debris blew out a back tire on the car, causing her to lose control and swerve into another lane on the highway. &ldquo;I was able to control my car. I was blessed,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;If you&rsquo;ve ever blown out your tire, you know it&rsquo;s really hard to control it.&rdquo; The damage to her vehicle &mdash; including a destroyed rim, tire and strut &mdash; cost more than $590. Potholes cost drivers, local municipalities and the state billions of dollars each year in damage repairs, insurance claims and civil complaints. The state has already used 8,818 tons of pavement patching material to repair potholes this year alone. That&rsquo;s up from the 6,823 tons last year and 4,178 tons used in 2015. Matt Bruning, spokesman for the Ohio Department of Transportation, said the cost of fixing each pothole varies &mdash; it could be hundreds of dollars to thousands of dollars. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll notice that we&rsquo;ve used a lot more material patching potholes over the past couple of years,&rdquo; Bruning said. &ldquo;While we&rsquo;re seeing more potholes, we&rsquo;re also doing a better job fixing them as quickly as we can.&rdquo; Gros said the pothole was fixed the next day after her accident, and that the crews had responded quickly and appropriately. The state has already spent $4.06 million to fill potholes. The department does not track data on how many potholes are repaired each year. &ldquo;If it is a more permanent fix, you will have a bigger crew, more equipment, more expensive asphalt,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;In the permanent fix you will cut out the damaged material. You make the cuts just outside of the damaged area, so that you get a good bond when you put in the new asphalt.&rdquo; Civil complaints can be filed against the state in the Ohio Court of Claims for pothole damage. If the pothole damage is $10,000 or less, drivers can file a complaint with the state court that was established in 1975 by the General Assembly. More than 1,000 Ohioans filed complaints in the past five years and were reimbursed nearly a half million dollars by the state. More than 125 civil complaints have been filed for vehicle damage because of potholes this year, according to a state database. That number is slightly down from a couple years ago when claims skyrocketed after a brutally cold winter with quickly swinging temperatures. Approximately 100 civil complaints related to potholes were filed in 2013, and that number spiked to 355 filed in 2015. Locally, some say the unseasonably warm winter conditions resulted in fewer potholes and damage vehicles &mdash; costing local municipalities and the state less in road repair and civil claims. Fred Stovall, public works director for Dayton, said there&rsquo;s been less this year &mdash; similar to trends seen with last year&rsquo;s mild winter as well. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re always going to have potholes,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s been a mild winter, and we do have some pot holes but it&rsquo;s nothing out of the norm.&rdquo; Potholes are typically caused by weather patterns. Through the winter months, water gets under the pavement and frees, then thaws, repeatedly. That expansion and contraction of water causes roadway material to break up. That action mixed with heavy traffic results in potholes. &ldquo;But potholes aren&rsquo;t just a winter problem,&rdquo; Bruning said. &ldquo;They can happen in warmer weather too. Water gets under the pavement, breaks down the asphalt and creates a void. Then the traffic on top creates depressions in the pavement.&rdquo; Potholes are most noticeable when the temperature starts to warm up again after cold weather, and crews usually have the busiest work time in January and February, according to the transportation department. Mark Breining, manager at Grismer Tire in Dayton, said it&rsquo;s been a slow year for vehicle damage due to potholes. The mild winter has kept more cars on the streets and out of auto repairs shops, he said. &ldquo;The snow was so minimal,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Everyone got a break from it this year.&rdquo; If a vehicle does hit a pothole, it can do extensive damage and cost upwards of hundreds of dollars for repair. A patch repair cost about $25, but a new wheel could cost up to $300 plus the price of a new tire. If the alignment or suspension of the car has been impacted, that will cost even more. While municipalities say there&rsquo;s not many ways to prevent potholes, one local company is working to analyze and disseminate pothole data. Founded by James Bridgers, Road-Aid uses geospatial and sensor, automatic target recognition on vehicles to identify where potholes are at &mdash; and then collect, analyze and inventory data about road issues. Bridgers said the goal is to better help consumers to understand where potholes are at, he said. And, governments can more readily &ldquo;deploy a fully proactive pothole identification program&rdquo; if they have the data to use. &ldquo;The average driver spends about $800 bucks a year just in vehicle maintenance due to poor road conditions- whether it&rsquo;s flat tires due to potholes or just normal bumpy roads,&rdquo; he told this newspaper. &ldquo;As they are driving along they will be notified and then actually have the image of where that pothole is so they can drive defensively and avoid it safety.&rdquo; &bull;&nbsp;Kroger to create 600 jobs with new Dayton-area stores &bull;&nbsp;RETAIL ROUND-UP: 3 stories about Cabela&rsquo;s, RadioShack, Starbucks &bull;&nbsp;Development officials see growth in downtown &bull;&nbsp;Centerville strip mall demolished next to Elsa&rsquo;s &bull;&nbsp;Ohio economy to win big with future NCAA games -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/business/potholes-costly-for-state-motorists/4VpDeKbdR1OJ85noNIaLKJ/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/DaytonDailyNews/2017/04/22/Images/newsEngin.18460386_IMG_0873.jpg\" title=\"Potholes costly for state, motorists\" alt=\"Potholes costly for state, motorists\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/business/potholes-costly-for-state-motorists/4VpDeKbdR1OJ85noNIaLKJ/\" target=\"\">Potholes costly for state, motorists</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nKara Driscoll</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word;
