League Baseball statistics, studies show that a 29-year-old centerfielder saves an average of four fewer runs per season than a 28-year-old centerfielder, The New York Times reported this week. While you may wonder why a prestigious newspaper would use valuable space to report stuff like that, baseball fans take such statistics seriously. And no statistic is too trivial to report. Somewhere in baseball&rsquo;s huge trove of baseball records, there may be one for &ldquo;Most errors committed by a left-handed shortstop born in Alaska.&rdquo; (This is a hyperbolic example. Only 11 of the 18,672 players in the history of baseball was born in Alaska, none of whom was a left-handed shortstop.) Talking about statistics gives fans something to do when nothing&rsquo;s happening on the field, which is most of the time. In an average three-hour game, according to one study, there are nine minutes and 45 seconds of actual activity. Unless you count players jogging to and from their dugouts as actual activity. MORE D.L.: Just &lsquo;plane&rsquo; carried away at the doctor&rsquo;s office. Now that most baseball fans in the stands are armed with cellphones, they don&rsquo;t have to wait to get home to their computers to savor the thrill of baseball statistics. Instead of watching a hitter stand in the batter&rsquo;s box and not swing at a pitch, they can Google the record for standing in a batter&rsquo;s box and not swinging at a pitch. Which, of course, was set last season by Bryce Harper of the Washington Nationals, who imitated a statue for seven consecutive at bats in a 13-inning game. Devout fans can use authentic statistics such as the following to engage in meaningful dialogues. Fan No. 1: &ldquo;Did you know Felix Hernandez is the only pitcher who made 16 consecutive starts, pitched at least seven innings and gave up two or fewer runs?&rdquo; Fan No. 2: &ldquo;Duh. But did you know that three pitchers who started 13 games and lasted fewer than seven games allowed at least three runs?&rdquo; READ MORE D.L.: A lifelong goal is inching away. The downside about getting engrossed in these statistics at the ballpark is that fans may not pay attention to the 9:45 of action on the field. In 1957, Philadelphia&rsquo;s Richie Ashburn set the record for most foul balls hitting the same fan (2); the first one struck spectator Alice Roth in the face, breaking her nose. As they carted her away for medical attention, Ashburn hit another foul ball, which struck the unfortunate Ms. Roth in the leg, breaking a bone in her knee. My guess is that she had been distracted by talking to the fan next to her about Cleveland&rsquo;s Bob Feller, who, in 1939, set the record for throwing the most pitches that were fouled into the stands, striking the pitcher&rsquo;s mother on Mother&rsquo;s Day (1). -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story \">\n\t    <div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/lifestyles/stewart-enjoying-major-league-baseball-the-numbers/foz6LoPk3sMmGBVlnQO3YK/\" target=\"\">D.L. the Elements of Good Cooking&rdquo; by Samin Nosrat (Simon and Schuster, 462 pages, $35). Hundreds of cookbooks are published each year. Some are good. Others are e,xceptional. A few are essential. Samin Nosrat just published &ldquo;Salt Fat Acid Heat &mdash; Mastering the Elements of Good Cooking&rdquo; and I daresay this one is essential. Cookbooks are laden with recipes &mdash; with that information we should be able to make dishes turn out well, right? The efforts we put into transforming recipes into flavorful dishes can sometimes feel like a waste of time. Foods that looked wonderful on the page don&rsquo;t always turn out as we might have expected. It really helps if we actually understand how to cook. That&rsquo;s the fabulous thing about this book &mdash; it teaches readers about cooking, how to employ various techniques, and how to grasp that any subtle variations in technique can have significant impacts upon our end results. It is possible to learn how to cook great food. As a child, Nosrat enjoyed the Persian dishes her family prepared. While attending college she had a meal at what is considered to be the best restaurant in America, Chez Panisse. That experience changed her life. She applied for a job there. As she observed and participated in the cooking taking place, she had a revelation. In the introduction to her book she reveals her realization that &ldquo;anyone can cook anything and make it delicious.&rdquo; MORE BOOK NOOK: A dozen great reasons to discover this series She explains, &ldquo;there are only four basic factors that determine how good your food will taste: salt, which enhances flavor; fat, which amplifies flavor and makes appealing textures possible; acid, which brightens and balances; and heat, which ultimately determines the texture of the food.&rdquo; After we fully comprehend that salt, fat, acid and heat are &ldquo;the four cardinal directions of cooking,&rdquo; we are ready to transform recipes and/or products of our own imaginations into appetizing dishes. We can build the confidence to cook on the fly while using the best ingredients available at the time, to improvise, to experiment, to really savor our flavor adventures. She goes into depth in each section &mdash; we delve into the science of what she is doing. For example, we learn about &ldquo;the Maillard reaction, heat&rsquo;s most significant contribution to flavor.&rdquo; When we employ acids in cooking the Maillard reaction can produce sour flavors. When we are using heat it will create savory scents and new textures. &ldquo;Salt Fat Acid Heat &mdash; Mastering the Elements of Good Cooking&rdquo; contains an abundance of material written in an approachable manner while remaining informative. Oh, and it is also filled with tempting recipes. Now that I feel hopeful that I have absorbed her food science and techniques, I&rsquo;m ready to embark on culinary expeditions. First I&rsquo;ll try out her recipe for slow-roasted salmon. This book is bound to become an indispensable addition to cookbook shelves throughout America. RELATED Love is the key ingredient in these recipes Dogs want you to read this cookbook now All are welcome at &ldquo;The Heavenly Table&rdquo; -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/entertainment/books--literature/the-book-nook-cookbook-that-can-teach-how-cook/SehCs0oGN6rIgZ8szxASpI/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/MyDaytonDailyNews/2017/04/22/Images/newsEngin.18349886_booknook042317.jpg\" title=\"THE BOOK NOOK: A cookbook that can teach us how to cook \" alt=\"THE BOOK NOOK: A cookbook that can teach us how to cook \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/entertainment/books--literature/the-book-nook-cookbook-that-can-teach-how-cook/SehCs0oGN6rIgZ8szxASpI/\" target=\"\">THE BOOK NOOK: A cookbook that can teach us how to cook </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nVick Mickunas, <span class=\"story-source\">Contributing Writer</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>“Salt Fat Acid Heat — Mastering the Elements of Good Cooking” by Samin Nosrat (Simon and Schuster, 462 pages, $35). Hundreds of cookbooks are published each year. Some are good. Others are e,xceptional. A few are essential. Samin Nosrat just published “Salt Fat Acid Heat — Mastering the Elements of Good Cooking” and I daresay this one is essential.
